9.2 The Snake-in-the-Box Problem
215
9.2
The Snake-in-the-Box Problem
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.2.1. The Hamming distance between two vertices in an n-cube is the
number of places in which the vertices‚Äô coordinates diÔ¨Äer. For a vertex Œ± in an
n-cube, the vertices that have Hamming distance of 1 to Œ± are called Œ±‚Äôs neighbours.
Example 9.2.2. The Hamming distance between 0001 and 1101 is 2.
The Hamming distance can be viewed as the length of the shortest path in the
cube to get from one vertex to the other one.
The snake-in-the-box problem in graph theory and computer science deals with
Ô¨Ånding a certain kind of path along the edges of an n-cube. This path starts at one
vertex and travels along the edges to as many vertices as it can reach. After it
visits a new vertex, the previous vertex and all of its neighbours must be marked
as unusable. The path should never travel to a vertex after it has been marked
unusable. In other words, two adjacent vertices in a path have Hamming distance
1, but for non-adjacent vertices, the Hamming distance must be at least 2 (the path
does not come too close to itself).
The snake-in-the-box problem was Ô¨Årst described by Kautz [86] in 1958, mo-
tivated by the theory of error-correcting codes.
The maximum lengths for the snake-in-the-box problem are known for dimen-
sions one through seven; these are
1, 2, 4, 7, 13, 26, 50.
Beyond the length n = 7, the exact length of the longest snake is not known;
the best lengths found so far for dimensions eight through twelve are
98, 190, 363, 680, 1260.
For the snake-in-the-box problem, it is known that as n goes to inÔ¨Ånity, the
maximum length is c2n for a constant c, 0 < c < 1. Thus, the maximum snake
is rather long since the total number of vertices in an n-cube is 2n. This result
was shown by Evdokimov [56] in 1969.
What makes this result special is that
combinatorics on words was crucial in obtaining it; thus, combinatorics on words
was helping graph theory in this case, while usually we witness the opposite situation
when these two disciplines meet (e.g. in the case of de Bruijn sequences, universal
permutations and word patterns discussed brieÔ¨Çy in Section 9.3 below).
The rest of this section is devoted to presenting the far from trivial construction
by Evdokimov [56] giving long snakes. We do not provide any proofs, nor known
upper and lower bounds for the constant c studied in the literature, whose exact
value is still unknown.
We begin by deÔ¨Åning the sequence of œÉ and the Zimin words.

216
Chapter 9. More on Words and Graphs in the Literature
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.2.3. Any natural number n can be presented unambiguously as n =
2t(4s + œÉ), where œÉ < 4, and t is the largest natural number such that 2t divides n.
If n runs through the natural numbers, then œÉ runs through the sequence wœÉ called
the sequence of œÉ. The initial letters of wœÉ are 11311331113313 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ .
Remark 9.2.4. The sequence wœÉ is equivalent to the Dragon curve sequence con-
sidered in the literature.
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.2.5. The nth Zimin word is deÔ¨Åned recursively as follows: Y1 = y1
and Yn = Yn‚àí1ynYn‚àí1.
Thus, Yn is over the alphabet {y1, y2, . . . , yn} and its length is easily seen by
mathematical induction to be 2n ‚àí1.
Example 9.2.6. The fourth Zimin word is Y4 = y1y2y1y3y1y2y1y4y1y2y1y3y1y2y1.
Zimin words appear in various contexts in the literature.
Let A and B be the preÔ¨Åxes of length 2k ‚àí1 of the sequences ababab ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ and
cdecdecde ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , respectively. Also, slightly abusing the notation, we assume now that
wœÉ denotes the preÔ¨Åx of length 2k ‚àí1 of the sequence wœÉ.
Further, let X = x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xk = (ac1)(bd1)(ae3)(bc1) ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , that is, xi can be
thought of as a ‚Äúsuper letter‚Äù formed by the ith letters of the sequences A, B and
wœÉ for 1 ‚â§i ‚â§2k ‚àí1. Clearly, some of the xis must be the same since the alphabet
of ‚Äúsuper letters‚Äù is limited to 12 letters (= 2 ¬∑ 3 ¬∑ 2). In fact, all 12 letters occur in
X for long enough k.
Next we shuÔ¨Ñe three sequences of length 2k‚àí1, namely X, Yk and Z = zz ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ z
as follows:
x1y1x2y2x3y1x4y3 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ (shuÔ¨Ñing X and Yk);
zx1zx2zx3zx4z ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ (shuÔ¨Ñing Z and X).
Finally, we form the word W by shuÔ¨Ñing the last two words:
W = x1zy1x1x2zy2x2x3zy1x3 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ .
The number of diÔ¨Äerent letters in the word W is n = 12 + k + 1 corresponding
to the dimension of an n-cube. The word W gives instructions on where to go in
the n-cube, that is, which bit to change to make the next move. It was proved
by Evdokimov [56] that W is ‚Äústrongly asymmetric‚Äù and the path corresponding
to it in the n-cube has desirable properties. Since the length of W is 2k+2 ‚àí4 =
2n‚àí11 ‚àí4 ‚âà0.0005 ¬∑ 2n, we obtain the desired result.

9.3 Universal Cycles and de Bruijn Graphs
217
9.3
Universal Cycles and de Bruijn Graphs
The notion of a universal object can be deÔ¨Åned in various ways depending on the
context. In Subsection 8.3.3 we discussed universal graphs and universal permu-
tations. The basic idea here is to come up with an object, typically of smallest
possible size, that contains inside it all the objects from a given class of objects.
The current section not only discusses the celebrated de Bruijn graphs along with
graphs of overlapping permutations, thus showing close relations between graphs
and words/permutations, but also provides a sketch of a general setup over univer-
sal cycles for combinatorial structures.
9.3.1
De Bruijn Graphs and Graphs of Overlapping Permu-
tations
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.3.1. An n-dimensional de Bruijn graph ‚ÉóDn,m is a directed graph on
mn vertices labelled by the words of length n over the m-letter alphabet Am =
{0, 1, . . . , m ‚àí1}. There is a directed edge from a vertex u to a vertex v if and only
if u = aw and v = wb for some word w over Am of length n ‚àí1, and a, b ‚ààAm, that
is, if the end of the word u overlaps with the beginning of v.
Example 9.3.2. See Figure 9.3 for de Bruijn graphs ‚ÉóD1,2, ‚ÉóD2,2 and ‚ÉóD3,2.
Figure 9.3: The graphs ‚ÉóD1,2, ‚ÉóD2,2 and ‚ÉóD3,2
Theorem 9.3.3. ‚ÉóDn,m is strongly connected and balanced, and thus by Theorem A.3.2
there exists an Eulerian cycle in ‚ÉóDn,m.
Proof. To show that ‚ÉóDn,m is strongly connected, we need to show that there is a
directed path in it from any vertex x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn to any other vertex y1y2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ yn. Such
00
11

01
1

000

218
Chapter 9. More on Words and Graphs in the Literature
a path is given by the following sequence of vertices:
x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn ‚Üíx2x3 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xny1 ‚Üíx3x4 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xny1y2 ‚Üí¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ‚Üíy1y2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ yn.
To show that ‚ÉóDn,m is balanced, we note that for any vertex x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn there
are exactly m arcs going in (all of the form xx1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn‚àí1 ‚Üíx1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn, x ‚ààAm)
and there are exactly m arcs coming out (all of the form x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn ‚Üíx2x3 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xnx,
x ‚ààAm).
The following theorem shows that ‚ÉóDn+1,m is the line graph of ‚ÉóDn,m. Recall the
deÔ¨Ånition of the line graph in DeÔ¨Ånition 2.2.27.
Theorem 9.3.4. ‚ÉóDn+1,m = L( ‚ÉóDn,m).
Proof. Label each edge x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn ‚Üíx2x3 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xnxn+1 in ‚ÉóDn,m by x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn+1. Then
it is not diÔ¨Écult to see that all arcs in ‚ÉóDn,m will be labelled in a bijective way by all
the words of length n + 1 over the alphabet Am. Finally, a directed path of length 2
x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn ‚Üíx2x3 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xnxn+1 ‚Üíx3x4 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xnxn+1xn+2
in ‚ÉóDn,m produces an edge x1x2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn+1 ‚Üíx2x3 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ xn+1xn+2, which is consistent with
the deÔ¨Ånition of ‚ÉóDn+1,m and completes the proof that ‚ÉóDn+1,m = L( ‚ÉóDn,m).
The existence of an Eulerian cycle in ‚ÉóDn,m shown in Theorem 9.3.3 gives the
existence of a Hamiltonian cycle in ‚ÉóDn+1,m due to Theorem 9.3.4. Following an
Eulerian cycle in ‚ÉóDn,m, we can write down a word corresponding to it that will
contain each word of length n over the alphabet Am exactly once as a factor.
Example 9.3.5. The word 0001011100 is obtained from ‚ÉóD3,2 following the Eulerian
cycle
000 ‚Üí001 ‚Üí010 ‚Üí101 ‚Üí011 ‚Üí111 ‚Üí110 ‚Üí100,
where we skipped recording the last step.
Thus, using graph theory we proved a result in combinatorics on words stating
the existence of sequences containing each word of given length over a given alphabet
as a factor exactly once. Such sequences are called de Bruijn sequences or, when the
universal word is read cyclicly, de Bruijn cycles.

9.3.2
Graphs of Overlapping Permutations
Graph of overlapping permutations are a permutation analogue of de Bruijn graphs.
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.3.6. A graph of overlapping permutations Pn has a directed edge for
each permutation œÄ = œÄ1œÄ2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ œÄn+1 ‚ààSn+1, the set of all permutations of length
n + 1, from red(œÄ1œÄ2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ œÄn) to red(œÄ2œÄ3 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ œÄn+1).
Example 9.3.7. There are two arcs from 2341 to 3412 in P4 labelled 24513 and
34512. Indeed, red(2451) = red(3451) = 2341 and red(4513) = red(4512) = 3412.
Also, see Figure 9.4 for the graph P2.
Graphs of overlapping permutations provide a useful tool for studying permu-
tations. These graphs are yet another good example of interrelations between words
(in this case permutations) and graphs.
9.3.3
Universal Cycles in Other Combinatorial Structures
Generalizing de Bruijn sequences/cycles considered in Subsection 9.3.1, we have the
following deÔ¨Ånition.
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.3.8. Given a family C of combinatorial objects that can be represented
by words of length n, a universal cycle, or U-cycle, for such a family is a word whose
length-n factors, read cyclically, represent all the elements of C without repetition.
Sometimes, the requirement for factors to be read cyclically is omitted, in which
case we can talk about universal words, or universal sequences.
Example 9.3.9. Example 9.3.5 gives a universal word for the set of all binary words
of length 3, which can be turned into a universal cycle for the same set by removing,
for example, the last two 0s: 00010111.
Chung et al. [37] extended the study of de Bruijn sequences to other combi-
natorial structures, such as the set of all permutations, the set of all partitions, and
the set of all subsets of a Ô¨Ånite set.

220
Chapter 9. More on Words and Graphs in the Literature
In the case of permutations, one needs to modify the notion of a U-cycle since
for n ‚â•3 it is not possible to list all n-permutations in the way we treated words.
Instead of looking at a factor of length n, we will look at the reduced form of the
factor. Then, our goal is to come up with a word, a U-cycle, such that each n-
permutation appears in it exactly once as a so-called consecutive pattern, that is, as
the reduced form of a factor. The existence of such U-cycles for any n was proved
in [37] by Chung et al. For example, for the set of length-3 permutations, such a
U-cycle is 145243. Indeed, reading the reduced forms cyclically starting from the
factor 145 we see the following permutations:
123 ‚Üí231 ‚Üí312 ‚Üí132 ‚Üí321 ‚Üí213.
Another U-cycle for the same set is 142342. To list all length-n permutations, we
need more than n diÔ¨Äerent letters. Chung et al. [37] conjectured that n + 1 letters
are always enough for constructing a U-cycle for the set of length-n permutations
for n ‚â•3. This conjecture was proved in [83] by Johnson.
The existence of U-cycles for all permutations was proved in [37] by showing
that the graph of overlapping permutations deÔ¨Åned in Subsection 9.3.2 has a Hamil-
tonian cycle. Similarly, Burstein and Kitaev [30] proved the existence of U-cycles
for all word patterns (which are the same as multi-permutations) by proving that a
certain graph contains a Hamiltonian cycle.
See [81] by Jackson et al. for a recent collection of research problems (and
references) on U-cycles.
9.4
Counting Independent Sets in Path-Schemes
Using Combinatorics on Words
Let G = (V, E) be a simple undirected graph with vertex set V = {1, . . . , n} and
set of edges E.
The cardinality of I(G), the set of all independent sets of G, was investigated
in the literature for various classes of graphs, see, for example, [32, 62, 87].
In
particular, Kitaev [87] used a classical result in combinatorics on words to Ô¨Ånd the
generating function for the number of independent sets on the class of well-based
path-schemes (see Theorem 9.4.14), which generalized a known result presented in
Theorem 9.4.7. In this section, we discuss the results in [87] following closely the
presentation in that paper.
Moreover, in Subsection 9.4.5 we discuss well-based
sequences associated with well-based path-schemes following [132] by Valyuzhenich.

9.4 Counting Independent Sets in Path-Schemes

|2 ‚àí6| = 4 ‚ààM while the fact that |2 ‚àí5| = 3 Ã∏‚ààM tells us that there is no edge
between the vertices 2 and 5.

9.4.5
On the Number of Well-Based Sequences
Theorem 9.4.14 gives a way to count independent sets in well-based path-schemes.
However, to know how useful this theorem is, we want to know how large the class of

226
Chapter 9. More on Words and Graphs in the Literature
well-based path-schemes is. Equivalently, how many diÔ¨Äerent well-based sequences
are there (see DeÔ¨Ånition 9.4.4)? While it does not seem to be feasible for the moment
to Ô¨Ånd an exact enumeration of the sequences, it is possible to Ô¨Ånd its asymptotic
growth. This subsection is based on the paper [132] by Valyuzhenich.
Clearly, in DeÔ¨Ånition 9.4.4 we can replace ‚Äúany number of 0s in Ai by 1s‚Äù by
‚Äúa 0 in Ai by 1‚Äù and still have an equivalent deÔ¨Ånition of a set. Thus, a well-based
sequence a1a2 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ can alternatively be deÔ¨Åned as follows: a1 = 1 and for any i ‚â•2,
if ai = t + s for non-negative integers t and s, then either t = aj or s = aj for some
j < i.
Let W(n) be the set of well-based sequences whose elements are at most n,
and let w(n) be the number of sequences in W(n).

DeÔ¨Ånition 9.4.18. A non-empty set B ‚äÜ{1, . . . , n} is a basis if no element x of B
is a non-negative integer linear combination of the elements of B ‚àí{x}. Let B(n)
be the set of all such Bs, and b(n) be the number of the elements in B(n).
Example 9.4.19. All bases for n = 4 are {1}, {2}, {3}, {4}, {2, 3} and {3, 4}.
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.4.20. A set S of integers is sum-free if for all a, b ‚ààS, a + b Ã∏‚ààS. The
family of all sum-free subsets S ‚äÜ{1, . . . , n} is denoted by S(n), and s(n) is the
number of elements in S(n).
Example 9.4.21. The set {3, 4, 8, 10} is sum-free while the set {3, 5, 6, 8, 10} is not
sum-free because of the elements 3, 5 and 8.

9.5 Clique-Width of Graphs
227
It was shown in [132, Theorem 1] that there is a one-to-one correspondence
between W(n) and B(n), so that w(n) = b(n). Moreover, the following discussion
was conducted in [132] (we refer to that paper for the respective references).
Note that any basis is a sum-free set. The converse to this statement does not
hold, since, in particular, any subset of odd numbers is a sum-free set, but if its
cardinality is at least 2, and it contains 1, then it is not a basis. Thus, B(n) ‚äÇS(n)
implying that w(n) = b(n) < s(n). Solving a conjecture of Cameron and ErdÀùos,
Green [71] and Sapozhenko [122] showed independently in 2003 that s(n) = O(2n/2).
Together with (9.3), we have the following result.
Theorem 9.4.22 ([132]). The asymptotics for w(n), the number of well-based se-
quences, is given by Œò(2n/2).
9.5
Clique-Width of Graphs
The notion of clique-width of a graph was introduced in [43] and is deÔ¨Åned as the
minimum number of labels needed to construct the graph by means of the four graph
operations:
‚Ä¢ i(v), the operation of creation of a vertex v with label i;
‚Ä¢ G ‚äïH, the disjoint union of two labelled graphs G and H;
‚Ä¢ Œ∑i,j, the operation that connects every vertex labelled i to every vertex la-
belled j;
‚Ä¢ œÅi‚Üíj, the operation that renames label i to label j.
Every graph can be deÔ¨Åned by an algebraic expression using the four operations
above. For instance, the graph consisting of two adjacent vertices x and y can be
deÔ¨Åned by the expression Œ∑1,2(1(x) ‚äï2(y)). Therefore, the notion of clique-width
provides one more way of describing graphs by words. Moreover, any graph can
be described by means of only the Ô¨Årst three operations. In the worst case, such a
description requires n labels for an n-vertex graph. However, for some graphs the
use of the fourth operation allows the number of labels used in the description to be
substantially reduced. For instance, it is not diÔ¨Écult to see that a path of any length
can be described with the help of three diÔ¨Äerent labels and a cycle of any length can
be described with the help of four diÔ¨Äerent labels. The following expression provides
such a description for a cycle C5 on vertices a, b, c, d, e (listed along the cycle):
Œ∑4,1(Œ∑4,3(4(e) ‚äïœÅ4‚Üí3(œÅ3‚Üí2(Œ∑4,3(4(d) ‚äïŒ∑3,2(3(c) ‚äïŒ∑2,1(2(b) ‚äï1(a)))))))).

228
Chapter 9. More on Words and Graphs in the Literature
An expression built from the above four operations is called a k-expression if
it uses k diÔ¨Äerent labels.
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.5.1. The clique-width of a graph G, denoted cwd(G), is the minimum
k such that there exists a k-expression deÔ¨Åning G.
From the above examples we conclude that the clique-width of any path is at
most 3 and the clique-width of any cycle is at most 4. The importance of the notion
of clique-width is due to the fact that many algorithmic graph problems that are
generally NP-hard admit polynomial-time solutions when restricted to graphs with
bounded clique-width [42].
It is known that graphs of clique-width at most 2 are precisely P4-free graphs
(see, e.g. [44]). The set of graphs of clique-width at most 3 is substantially richer
and includes, for instance, all forests. With k tending to inÔ¨Ånity, graphs of clique-
width at most k eventually cover all graphs, since the clique-width of any n-vertex
graph is at most n. However, for any Ô¨Åxed k, the class of graphs of clique-width at
most k is rather small and has at most factorial speed of growth deÔ¨Åned as follows.
DeÔ¨Ånition 9.5.2. A class of graphs X is said to be factorial if the number Xn of
n-vertex labelled graphs in X satisÔ¨Åes the inequalities nc1n ‚â§Xn ‚â§nc2n for some
constants c1 and c2.
In order to show that any class of graphs of bounded clique-width is at most
factorial, let us observe that any algebraic expression deÔ¨Åning a graph G can alter-
natively be represented as a rooted tree whose leaves correspond to the operations
of vertex creation, whose internal nodes correspond to the ‚äï-operations, and whose
root is associated with G. The operations Œ∑ and œÅ are assigned to the respective
edges of the tree. For example, the tree representing the expression above deÔ¨Åning
C5 is depicted in Figure 9.6. Notice that any expression deÔ¨Åning an n-vertex graph
contains exactly n ‚àí1 ‚äï-operations, and hence the corresponding tree has exactly
2n nodes.

We now use this representation together with the Pr¬®ufer sequence in order to

9.6 Conclusion
229
Theorem 9.5.3. For any integer k > 1, the class of graphs with clique-width at
most k, denoted by C(k), is factorial.
Proof. C(2) contains the class of co-graphs, which is known to be factorial (see, e.g.
[7]). This observation gives the lower bound. To obtain an upper bound on the num-
ber of graphs in C(k), we associate with each n-vertex graph G ‚ààC(k) a k-expression
F(G) of minimum length and the tree T(F(G)) representing F(G). The minimality
of F(G) ensures that the length of the label of any edge of the tree T(F(G)) is
bounded by a constant depending on k but not n. Consequently, T(F(G)) can be
encoded with a binary word of length O(n log2 n), which immediately implies the
required upper bound ncn on the number of graphs in C(k).
An encoding of T(F(G)) of the desired length can be obtained, for instance,
with the help of the Pr¬®ufer sequence. The tree T(F(G)) can easily be transformed
into a labelled tree by assigning the labels of the edges to the respective parent
nodes. Since T(F(G)) is a binary tree with 2n nodes and the length of any edge
label is bounded by a constant, we obtain a labelled tree with node labels of length
O(log2 n) (in binary representation), as required.
9.6
Conclusion
Quite often graphs are used as a tool for proving mathematical statements, in partic-
ular, statements in combinatorics on words. For example, Ô¨Ånding the Gray code can
be reduced to Ô¨Ånding a Hamiltonian path in an n-cube, while Ô¨Ånding de Bruijn se-
quences can be reduced to Ô¨Ånding a Hamiltonian cycle in a de Bruijn graph. On the
other hand, we do use combinatorics on words to prove statements in graph theory.
The current chapter gives three such examples, namely, Ô¨Ånding asymptotics for the
snake-in-the-box problem, Ô¨Ånding the number of independent sets of an arbitrary
well-based path-scheme, and Ô¨Ånding asymptotics for classes of graphs of bounded
degree (via Pr¬®ufer sequences).

Appendix A
Graph Theory Background
A graph G = (V, E) consists of two Ô¨Ånite sets V and E. The elements of V are
called the vertices and the elements of E the edges of G. Each edge is a pair of
vertices.
Graphs have natural graphical representations in which each vertex is rep-
resented by a point and each edge by a line connecting two points.
Figure A.1
represents the graph G = (V, E) with vertex set V = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5} and edge set
E = {12, 23, 34, 45}.
1
2
3
4
5
Figure A.1: Graph G = (V, E) with V = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5} and E = {12, 23, 34, 45}
By altering the deÔ¨Ånition, we can obtain diÔ¨Äerent types of graphs. For instance,
‚Ä¢ by replacing the set E with a set of ordered pairs of vertices, we obtain a
directed graph (digraph for short), also known as an oriented graph.
‚Ä¢ by allowing E to contain both directed and undirected edges, we obtain a
mixed graph.
‚Ä¢ by allowing repeated elements in the set of edges, i.e., by replacing E with a
multiset, we obtain a multigraph.
‚Ä¢ by allowing edges to connect a vertex to itself (a loop), we obtain a pseudo-
graph.
‚Ä¢ by allowing the edges to be arbitrary subsets of vertices, not necessarily of size
two, we obtain a hypergraph.

Appendix A. Graph Theory Background
‚Ä¢ by allowing V and E to be inÔ¨Ånite sets, we obtain an inÔ¨Ånite graph.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.0.1. A simple graph is a Ô¨Ånite undirected graph without loops and
multiple edges.
All graphs in this book are simple, unless stated otherwise.
A.1
Terminology and Notation
For a graph G, we denote by V (G) and E(G) the set of vertices and the set of edges
of G, respectively.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.1. Let u, v be two vertices of a graph G.
‚Ä¢ If uv ‚ààE(G), then u, v are said to be adjacent, in which case we also say that
u is connected to v or u is a neighbour of v. If uv Ã∏‚ààE(G), then u and v are
nonadjacent (not connected, non-neighbours).
‚Ä¢ The neighbourhood of a vertex v ‚ààV (G), denoted N(v), is the set of vertices
adjacent to v, i.e. N(v) = {u ‚ààV (G) | vu ‚ààE(G)}.
‚Ä¢ If e = uv is an edge of G, then e is incident to u and v. We also say that u
and v are the endpoints of e.
‚Ä¢ The degree of v ‚ààV (G), denoted deg(v), is the number of edges incident to v.
Alternatively, deg(v) = |N(v)|.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.2. The complement of a graph G = (V, E) is a graph with vertex
set V and edge set E‚Ä≤ such that e ‚ààE‚Ä≤ if and only if e Ã∏‚ààE. The complement of a
graph G is denoted G or co-G.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.3. Given two graphs G1 = (V1, E1) and G2 = (V2, E2), the graph
G1 is said to be
‚Ä¢ a subgraph of G2 if V1 ‚äÜV2 and E1 ‚äÜE2, i.e. G1 can be obtained from G2 by
deleting some vertices (with all incident edges) and some edges;
‚Ä¢ a spanning subgraph of G2 if V1 = V2, i.e. G1 can be obtained from G2 by
deleting some edges but not vertices;
‚Ä¢ an induced subgraph of G2 if every edge of G2 with both endpoints in V1 is
also an edge of G1, i.e. G1 can be obtained from G2 by deleting some vertices
(with all incident edges) but not edges.

A.1 Terminology and Notation
233
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.4. In a graph,
‚Ä¢ a walk is a sequence of (not necessarily distinct) vertices v1, v2, . . . , vk such
that vi is adjacent to vi+1 for each i = 1, . . . , k ‚àí1. A walk can alternatively
be described by the set of its edges v1v2, . . . , vk‚àí1vk. The length of a walk is
the number of its edges. A walk is closed if v1 = vk.
‚Ä¢ a trail is a walk with no repeated edges.
‚Ä¢ a path is a walk with no repeated vertices.
‚Ä¢ a Hamiltonian path is a path containing all vertices of the graph.
‚Ä¢ a cycle is a closed walk with no repeated vertices (except for v1 = vk).
‚Ä¢ a Hamiltonian cycle is a cycle containing all vertices of the graph.
‚Ä¢ an Eulerian cycle is a walk containing every edge of the graph exactly once.
‚Ä¢ the distance between two vertices a and b, denoted dist(a, b), is the length of
a shortest path joining them.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.5. A graph G is called connected if every pair of distinct vertices
is joined by a path.
Otherwise it is disconnected.
A maximal (with respect to
inclusion) connected subgraph of G is called a connected component of G.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.6. The diameter of a graph G is the maximum distance between
two of G‚Äôs vertices. A disconnected graph has inÔ¨Ånite diameter.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.7. Two graphs G1 = (V1, E1) and G2 = (V2, E2) are isomorphic if
there is a bijection f : V1 ‚ÜíV2 that preserves adjacency, i.e., uv ‚ààE1 if and only if
f(u)f(v) ‚ààE2.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.8. A graph G is self-complementary if G is isomorphic to its com-
plement.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.9. In a graph,
‚Ä¢ a clique is a set of pairwise adjacent vertices. The size of a maximum clique
in G is called the clique number of G and is denoted œâ(G).
‚Ä¢ an independent set (also known as a stable set) is a set of pairwise non-adjacent
vertices. The size of a maximum independent set in G is called the indepen-
dence number (also known as the stability number) of G and is denoted Œ±(G).

234
Appendix A. Graph Theory Background
‚Ä¢ a matching is a set of edges no two of which share a vertex. A matching is
perfect if it covers all vertices of the graph.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.10. A bipartite graph is a graph whose vertices can be divided into
two disjoint sets U and V such that every edge connects a vertex in U to one in V .
Some commonly used graphs:
‚Ä¢ Kn is the complete graph with n vertices, i.e. the graph with n vertices every
two of which are adjacent.
‚Ä¢ Kn,m is the complete bipartite graph, i.e. a bipartite graph with parts U and
V of size n and m, respectively, such that each vertex in U is connected to
every vertex in V .
‚Ä¢ On is the edgeless graph on n vertices, i.e. the graph on n vertices that has no
edges.
‚Ä¢ Pn is a chordless path with n vertices, i.e.
V (Pn) = {v1, v2, . . . , vn} and
E(Pn) = {v1v2, . . . , vn‚àí1vn}.
‚Ä¢ Cn is a chordless cycle with n vertices, i.e.
V (Cn) = {v1, v2, . . . , vn} and
E(Cn) = {v1v2, . . . , vn‚àí1vn, vnv1}.
‚Ä¢ Wn is a wheel, i.e. a graph obtained from a cycle Cn by adding a vertex which
is adjacent to every vertex of the cycle.
‚Ä¢ G + H is the union of two disjoint graphs G and H, i.e. if V (G) ‚à©V (H) = ‚àÖ,
then V (G+H) = V (G)‚à™V (H) and E(G+H) = E(G)‚à™E(H). In particular,
nG denotes the disjoint union of n copies of G.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.11. A star or star tree Sn is the complete bipartite graph K1,n
where n ‚â•0 and n = 0 corresponds to the single-vertex graph K1. The centrum of
a star is the all-adjacent vertex in it.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.1.12. The Ramsey number R(n, m) gives the solution to the party
problem, which asks the minimum number of guests that must be invited so that
at least m will know each other or at least n will not know each other. In the
language of graph theory, the Ramsey number is the minimum number of vertices
v = R(n, m) such that all undirected simple graphs of order v contain a clique of
order n or an independent set of order m.
The well known Ramsey‚Äôs Theorem states that such a number exists for all n
and m.

A.2
Directed Graphs
In this section, we present some terminology and notation for directed graphs. We
repeat that in a directed graph every edge is an ordered pair of vertices. Frequently,
directed edges are called arcs. Below, we represent an arc from a to b by (a, b),
although in the book, we also use the notation a ‚Üíb. Graphically, the orientation
of an arc is indicated by an arrow (see Figure A.2). Observe that two vertices of an
oriented graph can be connected by two arcs directed opposite to each other.
- 
- 

Figure A.2:
An oriented graph G = (V, E) with V

DeÔ¨Ånition A.2.1. Let G be a directed graph and v a vertex of G.
‚Ä¢ The in-degree of v is the number of edges directed to v, i.e. of the form (u, v).
‚Ä¢ The out-degree of v is the number of edges directed out of v, i.e. of the form
(v, u).
‚Ä¢ G is balanced if the in-degree equals the out-degree for each vertex of the graph.
DeÔ¨Ånition A.2.2. A directed graph is strongly connected if every vertex of the
graph is connected to every other vertex of the graph by a directed path (i.e. a path
that respects the orientation of the edges). A directed graph is weakly connected if
the underlying graph is connected.
A.3
Some Graph Theory Results Used in the Book
In this section we provide two well known results in graph theory.
Theorem A.3.1. A graph contains an Eulerian cycle if and only if the graph is
connected and the degree of each of its vertices is even.
Theorem A.3.2. A digraph contains an Eulerian cycle if and only if it is balanced
and strongly connected.

B.1
Algebra and Analysis
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.1. For sets A and B, the Cartesian product A √ó B is the set of all
ordered pairs (a, b) where a ‚ààA and b ‚ààB. That is,
A √ó B = {(a, b) | a ‚ààA and b ‚ààB}.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.2. Sn denotes the set of all permutations of length n. A permutation
of length n is normally thought of by us as a word of length n, without repeated
letters, over an n-letter alphabet, typically {1, 2, . . . , n}.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.3. A (non-strict) partial order is a binary relation ‚Äú‚â§‚Äù over a set P
which is reÔ¨Çexive, antisymmetric and transitive, i.e. which satisÔ¨Åes for all a, b and
c in P the following conditions:
‚Ä¢ a ‚â§a (reÔ¨Çexivity);
‚Ä¢ if a ‚â§b and b ‚â§a then a = b (antisymmetry);
‚Ä¢ if a ‚â§b and b ‚â§c then a ‚â§c (transitivity).
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.4. A set with a partial order is called a partially ordered set, or a
poset.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.5. For a, b, elements of a partially ordered set P, if a ‚â§b or b ‚â§a,
then a and b are comparable. Otherwise they are incomparable.

Appendix B. Non-graph-theoretical Background
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.6. A partial order under which every pair of elements is comparable
is called a total order or linear order. A totally ordered set is also called a chain.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.7. An element a is said to be covered by another element b if a is
strictly less than b and no third element c Ô¨Åts between them. That is, both a ‚â§b
and a Ã∏= b are true, and a ‚â§c ‚â§b is false for each c with a Ã∏= c Ã∏= b.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.8. A linear extension of a partial order is a linear order that is
compatible with the partial order.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.9. The dimension of a poset P is the least integer t for which there
exists a family of t linear extensions of P so that, for every x and y in P, x precedes
y in P if and only if it precedes y in each of the linear extensions.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.10. A family R = (‚â§1, . . . , ‚â§t) of linear orders on a set X is called
a realizer of a poset P = (X, ‚â§P) if ‚â§p= ‚à©R which is to say that for any x and y
in X, x ‚â§P y precisely when x ‚â§1 y, x ‚â§2 y, . . ., and x ‚â§t y.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.11. Suppose that S is a set and ¬∑ is some binary operation S √óS ‚Üí
S, then S with ¬∑ is a monoid if it satisÔ¨Åes the following two axioms:
‚Ä¢ Associativity. For all a, b and c in S, the equation (a ¬∑ b) ¬∑ c = a ¬∑ (b ¬∑ c) holds.
‚Ä¢ Identity element. There exists an elenent e in S such that for every element
a in S, the equations e ¬∑ a = a ¬∑ e = a hold.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.12. For a set A, the set of all words over A, denoted A‚àó, is called
the free monoid.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.13. Given a set A, the free commutative monoid on A, denoted
[A], is the set of all Ô¨Ånite multisets with elements drawn from A, with the monoid
operation being multiset sum and the monoid unit being the empty multiset.
A morphism is a structure-preserving map from one structure to another. A
special case of a morphism is deÔ¨Åned next.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.14. A map f : X ‚ÜíY is called an epimorphism if g1 ¬∑ f = g2 ¬∑ f
implies g1 = g2 for all morphisms g1, g2 : Y ‚ÜíZ.
In the following deÔ¨Ånition, one may think of a formal power series as a power
series in which we ignore questions of convergence by not assuming that the variable
denotes any numerical value.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.15. A generating function is a formal power series in one indeter-

We end this section by presenting three members of the family of Bachmann-

DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.16 (Big-O notation). f(x) = O(g(x)) means that there exists a

DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.17 (Small-O notation). f(x) = o(g(x)) means that for all c > 0
there exists some k > 0 such that 0 ‚â§f(n) < c ¬∑ g(n) for all n ‚â•k. The value of k
must not depend on n, but may depend on c.
DeÔ¨Ånition B.1.18 (Big-Theta notation). f(n) = Œò(g(n)) if f is bounded both
above and below by g asymptotically. That is, there exist positive k1, k2 and n0
such that k1 ¬∑ g(n) ‚â§f(n) ‚â§k2 ¬∑ g(n) for all n > n0.

DeÔ¨Ånition B.2.4. The nth Bell number Bn counts the number of diÔ¨Äerent ways to
partition a set with n elements.
The Ô¨Årst few Bell numbers, for n = 0, 1, 2, . . ., are
1, 1, 2, 5, 15, 52, 203, 877, 4140, 21147, 115975, . . . .
The following proposition is known as the pigeonhole principle. Despite being
trivial, the principle has found many applications in various problems, e.g. in proving
that the maximum number of non-attacking knights on the chessboard is 32.
Proposition B.2.5 (The pigeonhole principle). If n items are put into m contain-
ers, with n > m, then at least one container must contain more than one item.
